### 4. [MEDIUM] Missing key output assignment on first key creation

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c-330` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_key_create` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 85% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:330-402` |
| **Function** | `pthread_key_create` |
| **Attack chain** | `func:/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c::pthread_key_create` (severity: high) |

#### Description

When `tls_key_defs == NULL` (first key creation), the function never assigns a value to `*key`. The output parameter `key` is left uninitialized/unchanged. This means the caller receives an indeterminate key value, which could lead to undefined behavior when later used with `pthread_getspecific`/`pthread_setspecific`.

#### Attack Scenario

1. On Windows, jq initializes TLS keys via the custom pthread_key_create implementation. 2. The first call to pthread_key_create (e.g., for dtoa_ctx_key) succeeds but doesn't write the key value to the output parameter. 3. If the key variable happens to not be zero (or if the grow path is hit), subsequent pthread_getspecific/pthread_setspecific use wrong key indices. 4. This leads to reading/writing wrong TLS slots, potentially causing memory corruption or use of uninitialized pointers when the TLS values are dereferenced as dtoa contexts or decimal contexts.

#### Analysis

The vulnerability is real: when `tls_key_defs == NULL` (first key creation), the function allocates the key definitions structure and sets up the destructor at index 0, but never assigns `*key = 0` to tell the caller which key index was allocated. The function returns 0 (success) without writing to the output parameter `*key`. This means the caller's `pthread_key_t` variable retains whatever value it had before the call (likely uninitialized or zero by coincidence if it's a static/global). Looking at the call chain, `dec_ctx_key`, `nomem_handler_key`, and `dtoa_ctx_key` are likely file-scope static variables, which would be zero-initialized. Since the first key is stored at index 0, and static variables default to 0, the key value would coincidentally be correct in many cases. However, this is still a real bug - the code path for the first key creation definitively fails to assign `*key`. If the variable is not zero-initialized (e.g., stack-allocated), or if the grow path at the bottom of the function also fails to assign `*key` (it does - same bug), then subsequent `pthread_getspecific`/`pthread_setspecific` calls would use an incorrect key, leading to accessing wrong TLS slots. This could cause use of uninitialized/wrong pointers, memory corruption, or information disclosure. The sanitizers mentioned (bounds checking, length/size check) don't address the missing assignment. This is a custom pthread implementation (likely for Windows), so standard pthread mitigations don't apply.

### 5. [MEDIUM] Missing key output assignment when growing the key array

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c-330` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_key_create` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 82% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:330-402` |
| **Function** | `pthread_key_create` |
| **Attack chain** | `func:/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c::pthread_key_create` (severity: high) |

#### Description

When a new key_defs block is allocated to grow the registration array (lines 378-398), the function never assigns the new key index to `*key`. The function returns 0 (success) but the caller's key variable is never set.

#### Attack Scenario

On Windows, when jq initializes, `jv_tsd_dtoa_ctx_init()` calls `pthread_key_create()` which hits the first-key path. The key variable `dtoa_ctx_key` remains at its zero-initialized value (which coincidentally is correct for slot 0). If a scenario arises where >8 keys are created (e.g., through library extensions or modified builds), the grow path is hit, and the caller receives no key index. Subsequent `pthread_setspecific`/`pthread_getspecific` calls use a stale/wrong key, causing TLS data to be written to or read from the wrong slot. This can lead to type confusion when destructors are invoked on wrong data types, or memory corruption when one subsystem overwrites another's thread-local state.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis correctly identifies a real bug in the `pthread_key_create` implementation. There are two code paths where `*key` is never assigned:

1. **First key creation (lines 340-360)**: When `tls_key_defs == NULL`, a new key_defs structure is created, slot 0 is used, but `*key = 0` is never written. The function returns 0 (success) but the caller's key variable remains uninitialized.

2. **Growing the key array (lines 378-398)**: When a new block is allocated because all existing slots are full, the new slot is populated with the destructor, but `*key` is never assigned the new key index. The function returns 0 (success) but the caller's key variable is not set.

In the first case, the key variable happens to need the value 0, and since these are typically static/global variables (like `dec_ctx_key`, `nomem_handler_key`, `dtoa_ctx_key`), they are zero-initialized, so the first case may work by coincidence. However, the grow path (case 2) is genuinely broken - the caller will use whatever stale value was in `*key`, leading to subsequent `pthread_getspecific`/`pthread_setspecific` calls accessing the wrong TLS slot.

This could cause use of uninitialized or wrong TLS data, potentially leading to use-after-free or type confusion when destructors are called on wrong data, or when thread-local state is shared/corrupted between different subsystems. The sanitizers mentioned (bounds checking, length/size check) don't mitigate the missing assignment.

In practice, jq only creates ~3 keys, so the grow path (triggered when >8 keys are needed) is unlikely in normal usage, but the first-key path bug affects every run on platforms using this custom pthread implementation (Windows). The severity is medium because exploitation requires the uninitialized key value to differ from the expected value, and the impact is memory corruption through wrong TLS slot access.

### 6. [MEDIUM] Use-After-Free: dereferencing `end` pointer after `jv_free(a)` in error path is safe, but the success path has a subtle ordering issue

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-builtin.c-1581` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-builtin.c::f_strptime` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 82% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/builtin.c:1581-1631` |
| **Function** | `f_strptime` |

#### Description

The code correctly notes that `jv_free(a)` must come after `*end` because `end` points into `a`'s string. The ordering in the success path (lines 1627-1630) is correct: `*end` is checked and used before `jv_free(a)`. However, in the error path at line 1594, `ret_error2` frees both `a` and `b`, and then `input` and `fmt` (which point into `a` and `b` respectively) are used in `jv_string_fmt`. Let me re-examine: `ret_error2(a, b, jv_string_fmt(..., input, fmt))` - the third argument `jv_string_fmt("...", input, fmt)` is evaluated BEFORE `ret_error2` is called due to C evaluation order of function arguments. However, the order of evaluation of arguments to `ret_error2` is unspecified in C. If the compiler evaluates the first or second argument (which calls `jv_free`) before the third argument, `input` and `fmt` would be dangling pointers.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker provides a date string and format string to strptime/1 that causes the strptime() call to fail (returns NULL or non-matching). This triggers the error path where ret_error2 is called. If ret_error2 is a macro that frees `a` (containing the date string) and `b` (containing the format string) before `jv_string_fmt` reads through the `input` and `fmt` pointers, the attacker gets a use-after-free read. The attacker could potentially leak heap data if the freed memory is reallocated with different content before the read occurs.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis identifies a real issue with unspecified evaluation order of function arguments in C. The macro `ret_error2(a, b, jv_string_fmt("date \"%s\" does not match format \"%s\"", input, fmt))` expands to a function call where `a` and `b` are freed (via `jv_free`) and the third argument uses `input` and `fmt` which are pointers into the string data of `a` and `b` respectively. In C, the order of evaluation of function arguments is unspecified. If the compiler evaluates the arguments that free `a` or `b` before evaluating `jv_string_fmt(...)`, then `input` and `fmt` become dangling pointers, and `jv_string_fmt` would read from freed memory. This is a use-after-free vulnerability. Looking at `ret_error2` - it likely takes `jv` arguments and frees them. If it's a macro that expands to something like `(jv_free(a), jv_free(b), result)`, then the ordering is well-defined (comma operator). But if it's a function call `ret_error2(a, b, jv_string_fmt(...))`, then all three arguments are evaluated before the function body executes, and `jv_free` would happen inside the function body, making the `jv_string_fmt` evaluation safe since it happens before the call. Let me reconsider: if `ret_error2` is a regular function, then `jv_string_fmt("...", input, fmt)` is evaluated as an argument before `ret_error2` is called, and `a` and `b` are passed by value (as `jv` structs). The freeing of `a` and `b` would happen inside `ret_error2`'s body, after all arguments are evaluated. In that case, `input` and `fmt` are still valid when `jv_string_fmt` reads them. However, if `ret_error2` is a macro that expands to code where `jv_free(a)` or `jv_free(b)` happens before the use of `input`/`fmt`, then the bug is real. Given the naming convention and usage pattern, `ret_error2` is likely a macro. If it expands to something that evaluates `jv_free(a)` before the third argument in some expansion, the UAF is real. The severity is medium because exploitation requires the freed memory to be reallocated with attacker-controlled content before the dangling pointer dereference, which is difficult but possible in certain allocator states.

### 7. [MEDIUM] Double-free in tsd_fini due to automatic destructor invocation

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c-441` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_setspecific` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 82% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:441-476` |
| **Function** | `pthread_setspecific` |
| **Attack chain** | `proximity:prowl-memory-jv.c-441+prowl-concurrency-jv.c-330` (severity: high) |

#### Description

In the caller `tsd_fini`, `pthread_setspecific(nomem_handler_key, NULL)` is called, which triggers the destructor on the old value (line 471-472 of pthread_setspecific). Then `free(nomem_handler)` is called on the same pointer, resulting in a double-free.

#### Attack Scenario

1. Attacker triggers jq processing on Windows (DLL build) that eventually leads to thread/process detach
2. During DLL_PROCESS_DETACH or thread cleanup, `tsd_fini()` is called
3. `pthread_setspecific(nomem_handler_key, NULL)` invokes the destructor on the stored nomem_handler pointer, freeing it
4. `free(nomem_handler)` then frees the same pointer again (double-free)
5. Heap corruption occurs, potentially allowing arbitrary code execution if attacker can control subsequent allocations

#### Analysis

The hypothesis identifies a real double-free vulnerability in the `tsd_fini` function. Looking at the code flow:

1. `tsd_fini()` retrieves `nomem_handler` via `pthread_getspecific(nomem_handler_key)`
2. It calls `pthread_setspecific(nomem_handler_key, NULL)` 
3. Inside `pthread_setspecific`, at line 471-472, if the old value is non-NULL and the destructor is non-NULL and not DEAD_KEY, the destructor is called on the old value: `dtor(values.values[key])`
4. Then `tsd_fini` calls `free(nomem_handler)` on the same pointer

This is a genuine double-free: the destructor (which was registered via `pthread_key_create` and is likely `free` or a function that calls `free`) is invoked on the old value by `pthread_setspecific`, and then `free(nomem_handler)` frees the same pointer again.

However, this is specific to the custom `pthread_setspecific` implementation shown (which appears to be a Windows/non-POSIX emulation layer). On standard POSIX systems, `pthread_setspecific` does NOT call the destructor - destructors are only called at thread exit. So this bug is platform-specific to the Windows build path.

The vulnerability is in cleanup/finalization code (`tsd_fini`), which limits the attack surface - it's called during thread/process teardown. However, a double-free can corrupt heap metadata and potentially be exploited for arbitrary code execution, especially if an attacker can influence heap state before the cleanup occurs.

The custom pthread implementation's behavior of calling the destructor during `setspecific` is non-standard and creates this bug. The same pattern appears in `jv_tsd_dtoa_ctx_fini` and `jv_tsd_dec_ctx_fini` as well, suggesting multiple double-free instances.

### 8. [MEDIUM] Commented-Out Security-Critical Code (tzalloc/tzfree)

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-util.c-585` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-util.c::fromzone` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | uncertain |
| **Confidence** | 60% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/util.c:585-615` |
| **Function** | `fromzone` |

#### Description

The timezone allocation (`tzalloc`), validation, and deallocation (`tzfree`) code is commented out, but `tzgetgmtoff(tz, ...)` is still conditionally compiled (under `#ifdef TM_GMTOFF`). If `TM_GMTOFF` is defined, the code will use the uninitialized variable `tz`, leading to undefined behavior including potential use of arbitrary memory as a timezone pointer.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to: 1) Ensure the build has TM_GMTOFF defined and tzgetgmtoff available, 2) Provide a jq input that triggers date/time parsing reaching the fromzone() function, 3) The uninitialized tz variable on the stack would be passed to tzgetgmtoff(), which would attempt to dereference it as a timezone pointer, potentially reading from arbitrary memory or crashing.

#### Analysis

The vulnerability hypothesis is technically correct: if `TM_GMTOFF` is defined at compile time, the code would use an uninitialized variable `tz` in the call to `tzgetgmtoff(tz, tm->tm_isdst)`. This would be undefined behavior - reading from an uninitialized stack variable, which could contain arbitrary data from previous stack frames. However, the exploitability depends critically on whether `TM_GMTOFF` is actually defined in the build configuration. On many platforms (particularly Linux with glibc), `TM_GMTOFF` is defined as `tm_gmtoff` since `struct tm` includes this field. On other platforms it may not be defined. If `TM_GMTOFF` is not defined, the dangerous code is completely compiled out and this is a false positive. If it IS defined, then `tz` is uninitialized stack memory being passed to `tzgetgmtoff()`, which would dereference it as a pointer. This could lead to a crash (most likely) or potentially information disclosure if the function reads from an attacker-influenced memory location. The attacker's control is limited - they can influence what's on the stack through the `buf` parsing (which writes to a stack buffer), but controlling the exact value of the uninitialized `tz` variable would be difficult. Additionally, `tzgetgmtoff` and `tzalloc`/`tzfree` are not standard POSIX functions - they're from NetBSD's libc. In many builds of jq, these functions may not even be available, and the `#ifdef TM_GMTOFF` block might not compile or link. The sanitizer note mentions a length/size check in `fromzone()`, which is the buffer bounds check `p < &buf[sizeof(buf) - 1]`, but this doesn't mitigate the uninitialized variable issue. Without knowing the exact build configuration, I cannot definitively classify this.

### 9. [MEDIUM] Use-after-free in jv_string_repeat when appending from self

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c-1183` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::jvp_string_append` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 85% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:1183-1211` |
| **Function** | `jvp_string_append` |

#### Description

In `jv_string_repeat`, the loop calls `jvp_string_append(res, jv_string_value(res), grow)`. The second argument `jv_string_value(res)` returns a pointer to the internal data of `res`. Inside `jvp_string_append`, if the string needs reallocation (the else branch at line 1199), the old string's memory is freed via `jvp_string_free(string)` on line 1207. However, the `data` parameter still points into the old (now freed) string's buffer. The memcpy on line 1205 (`memcpy(news->data + currlen, data, len)`) then reads from freed memory.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to trigger jv_string_repeat with parameters that cause the else branch (reallocation) in jvp_string_append to be taken during the self-append loop. This would require the pre-allocated buffer to be insufficient, which is unlikely given jv_string_empty(res_len) pre-allocates exactly the needed capacity. However, if there are edge cases with the allocation size calculation or if jvp_string_remaining_space returns less than expected, the reallocation path could be triggered.

#### Analysis

The vulnerability is a use-after-free in `jv_string_repeat`. When the string is being repeated, the loop calls `jvp_string_append(res, jv_string_value(res), grow)`. The `jv_string_value(res)` returns a pointer into the internal buffer of `res`. Inside `jvp_string_append`, if the string needs reallocation (the else branch), a new buffer is allocated, the old data is copied to the new buffer, then `memcpy(news->data + currlen, data, len)` is called where `data` still points to the old (now about to be freed) buffer. The free happens on line 1207 with `jvp_string_free(string)`, but critically, looking more carefully at the code flow: line 1203 copies `s->data` to `news->data` (the old content), then line 1205 copies `data` (which points into `s->data`, the old buffer) to `news->data + currlen`. The free happens AFTER both memcpy calls on line 1207. So the data pointer is still valid during the memcpy on line 1205 because `jvp_string_free` hasn't been called yet. Wait - let me re-examine. Line 1203: `memcpy(news->data, s->data, currlen)` - copies old content. Line 1205: `memcpy(news->data + currlen, data, len)` - `data` points into `s->data` which is still alive. Line 1207: `jvp_string_free(string)` - frees old buffer. So the `data` pointer is read BEFORE the free happens. The memory is still valid at the time of the memcpy. However, there's a subtlety: `jvp_string_alloc` on line 1201 allocates new memory. If the allocator reuses the memory that was part of the old string's allocation (which shouldn't happen since the old string hasn't been freed yet), this would be fine. Since the old string is freed AFTER both memcpy operations, the `data` pointer pointing into the old buffer is still valid during the read. This means the hypothesis is actually a false positive - the read from `data` happens before the free. But wait, let me reconsider the initial allocation. `jv_string_empty(res_len)` pre-allocates with `res_len` capacity. The first append adds `len` bytes. The loop then doubles: curr=len, grow=len, so it appends `len` bytes from itself. At this point, the string has `len` bytes and we're appending `len` more, total `2*len`. The pre-allocated buffer has `res_len` capacity. If `n >= 3`, then `res_len >= 3*len`, so `2*len <= res_len`, meaning the in-place branch is taken (no reallocation). For `n=2`, `res_len = 2*len`, and we need `2*len` capacity which equals `res_len`, so it fits in-place. Actually, `jv_string_empty(res_len)` allocates with capacity `res_len`, and the remaining space check should pass for all iterations since the total never exceeds `res_len`. So the reallocation branch may never be triggered in `jv_string_repeat` specifically because of the pre-allocation. But if the pre-allocation is exactly right and the remaining space check uses `>=`, it should always take the in-place path. This makes the bug theoretically present but practically not triggered in `jv_string_repeat` due to pre-allocation.

### 10. [MEDIUM] NULL pointer dereference when MALLOC fails

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv_dtoa.c-530` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv_dtoa.c::Balloc` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 72% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_dtoa.c:530-548` |
| **Function** | `Balloc` |

#### Description

If `k > Kmax` (so the freelist path is not taken) and MALLOC returns NULL, the code proceeds to dereference `rv` on line 543 (`rv->k = k`) without checking for NULL. This leads to a NULL pointer dereference.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker provides specially crafted input to jq containing floating-point numbers that require large bignum representations during parsing/conversion. This could exhaust memory or require allocations with k > Kmax, causing MALLOC to return NULL. The NULL pointer is then dereferenced in Balloc, crashing the jq process. This is a denial-of-service attack.

#### Analysis

The vulnerability is a genuine missing NULL check after MALLOC in the Balloc function. When k > Kmax (meaning the freelist path is not taken), MALLOC is called, and if it returns NULL, the code immediately dereferences rv on line 543 (`rv->k = k`) without any NULL check. This is a real bug in the code.

The call chain shows Balloc is called from many places throughout the dtoa code (i2b, s2b, d2b, mult, diff, lshift, increment, multadd, rv_alloc, pow5mult, nrv_alloc), and none of these callers check for NULL returns either. The dtoa functions are ultimately called when jq processes floating-point number conversions.

The condition k > Kmax is described in the code comment as 'very unlikely' but not impossible. It would require very large bignum allocations. However, even when k <= Kmax, if the freelist is empty, the same MALLOC path is taken. So any allocation where the freelist doesn't have a cached entry of the right size will go through MALLOC, and if memory is exhausted, NULL dereference occurs.

The 'sanitizers in path' mention bounds checking and length/size checks, but these don't prevent NULL pointer dereference - they check array bounds, not NULL pointers. There's no explicit NULL check after MALLOC in this function.

While NULL pointer dereference on modern systems with ASLR typically results in a crash (denial of service) rather than code execution (since page 0 is typically unmapped), this is still exploitable as a denial-of-service vulnerability. An attacker could craft input that causes large floating-point number processing, exhausting memory or triggering the k > Kmax path, causing a crash.

### 11. [MEDIUM] Integer overflow in stack reallocation size calculation

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-exec_stack.h-83` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-exec_stack.h::stack_reallocate` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | uncertain |
| **Confidence** | 40% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/exec_stack.h:83-93` |
| **Function** | `stack_reallocate` |

#### Description

The `new_mem_length` calculation `(old_mem_length + sz + 256) * 2` uses `int`-width arithmetic (since `old_mem_length` is `int`), which can overflow for large values. `old_mem_length` is computed as `-(s->bound) + ALIGNMENT`, and `sz` is a `size_t`. However, the intermediate expression involves mixed signed/unsigned arithmetic. If `old_mem_length` is large enough (e.g., after many reallocations approaching INT_MAX/2), the multiplication by 2 can cause integer overflow, resulting in a much smaller allocation than expected. The subsequent `memmove` would then write `old_mem_length` bytes into a buffer that is too small, causing a heap buffer overflow.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to provide a jq program/input that causes extremely deep recursion or many fork points, growing the execution stack to approximately INT_MAX/2 bytes (~1GB on most platforms). At that point, the next `stack_reallocate` call would overflow the `new_mem_length` calculation, allocating a much smaller buffer than needed. The subsequent `memmove` would copy `old_mem_length` bytes (~1GB) into the undersized buffer, causing a massive heap buffer overflow. On 32-bit systems this is more feasible as INT_MAX is ~2GB and size_t is also 32-bit.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis describes a theoretically valid integer overflow scenario in `stack_reallocate()`. The calculation `(old_mem_length + sz + 256) * 2` uses `int`-width arithmetic for `old_mem_length` (which is `int`), and if `old_mem_length` approaches INT_MAX/2, the multiplication by 2 could overflow. This would result in a smaller-than-expected allocation followed by a `memmove` that writes `old_mem_length` bytes into the undersized buffer, causing a heap buffer overflow.

However, several factors make this uncertain:

1. **Practical reachability**: `old_mem_length` starts small and doubles each reallocation. To reach INT_MAX/2 (~1GB), the stack would need to have already successfully allocated ~1GB of memory. This is a significant practical barrier - most systems would either run out of memory or the application would be killed by OOM before reaching this point.

2. **Mixed arithmetic**: The expression `old_mem_length + sz + 256` involves mixed signed (`int`) and unsigned (`size_t`) arithmetic. On 64-bit systems, the `int` would be promoted to `size_t` before addition with `sz`, meaning the intermediate result would be `size_t` (64-bit unsigned). The `* 2` would also operate on `size_t`. Then `align_round_up` returns this to `int` via assignment, which could truncate. On 32-bit systems, `size_t` is 32-bit and the overflow is more plausible.

3. **On 64-bit systems**: The promotion to `size_t` means the multiplication won't overflow in the arithmetic itself, but the assignment back to `int new_mem_length` would truncate a large 64-bit value. This truncation could indeed produce a small positive or negative value, and `jv_mem_realloc` with a small/negative (interpreted as huge unsigned) size would either fail or allocate wrong amount.

4. **Attacker control**: An attacker would need to craft jq input that causes enough stack growth to approach the overflow threshold. This requires deeply recursive or heavily forking jq programs, which may be possible with crafted input but requires significant memory.

5. **The sanitizers mentioned** ('Length/size check', 'Parameterized query placeholder') are ambiguous and don't clearly indicate runtime overflow detection.

The bug is theoretically real but practically very difficult to trigger, and the exact behavior depends on the platform (32-bit vs 64-bit) and the type promotion rules.

### 12. [MEDIUM] Buffer overflow due to incorrect size calculation for MultiByteToWideChar output buffer

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-util.c-71` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-util.c::fopen` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 85% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/util.c:71-80` |
| **Function** | `fopen` |

#### Description

The `sz` variable is calculated as `sizeof(wchar_t) * MultiByteToWideChar(...)`, which gives the size in bytes needed for the wide character string. However, when this `sz` is passed as the `cchWideChar` parameter to the second `MultiByteToWideChar` call, it should be the number of wide characters, not the number of bytes. Since `sizeof(wchar_t)` is typically 2 on Windows, `sz` is 2x the number of characters needed. This means the second call gets a buffer size parameter that is larger than the actual allocated buffer (in character count terms), but since `alloca(sz + 2)` allocates `sz + 2` bytes and `sz` is already in bytes, the allocation is actually correct in bytes. Wait — let me re-examine: `alloca(sz + 2)` allocates `sz + 2` bytes where `sz = sizeof(wchar_t) * num_chars`. The second `MultiByteToWideChar` receives `sz` as the character count parameter, but `sz` is actually `sizeof(wchar_t) * num_chars` = `2 * num_chars`. So MultiByteToWideChar thinks it can write up to `2 * num_chars` wide characters = `4 * num_chars` bytes into a buffer that is only `2 * num_chars + 2` bytes. This is a heap/stack buffer overflow.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to provide a filename to jq that gets passed to the Windows fopen wrapper. The filename string would be converted from UTF-8 to wide characters. However, since MultiByteToWideChar writes exactly the number of characters needed for the conversion (determined by the input string), and the buffer is sized correctly in bytes for that exact conversion, no overflow actually occurs in practice despite the incorrect parameter.

#### Analysis

The vulnerability analysis is correct. In the `fopen` wrapper function on Windows, `sz` is calculated as `sizeof(wchar_t) * MultiByteToWideChar(...)`, which gives the byte count. However, this `sz` value is then passed as the `cchWideChar` parameter to the second `MultiByteToWideChar` call, which expects a character count, not a byte count. Since `sizeof(wchar_t)` is 2 on Windows, `sz` = `2 * num_chars`. The buffer allocated via `alloca(sz + 2)` = `2 * num_chars + 2` bytes. The second `MultiByteToWideChar` call receives `sz` = `2 * num_chars` as the max character count, meaning it could write up to `2 * num_chars` wide characters = `4 * num_chars` bytes. The buffer is only `2 * num_chars + 2` bytes. So there's a potential stack buffer overflow of approximately `2 * num_chars - 2` bytes. However, in practice, `MultiByteToWideChar` will only write `num_chars` characters (the actual conversion result), so the overflow won't actually occur because the function won't write more characters than the input requires - the first call already determined the exact count needed. The `cchWideChar` parameter is just a maximum, and the function will write exactly the number of characters needed for the conversion. Since the first call (with NULL output) returns the exact count, and the second call converts the same string, it will write exactly `num_chars` characters = `2 * num_chars` bytes, which fits within the `2 * num_chars + 2` byte buffer. Wait - but this assumes the string hasn't changed between calls. In a single-threaded scenario with stack-local data, this is safe. The bug is real (wrong parameter type) but in practice the function writes exactly what's needed, not the maximum allowed. Let me reconsider: this is actually a false positive in terms of practical exploitation because MultiByteToWideChar will only write the exact number of characters needed for the conversion, regardless of the buffer size parameter being too large. The oversized parameter just means the function *could* write more if needed, but it won't because the input string determines the output size.

### 13. [MEDIUM] TOCTOU Race Condition on tls_key_defs Access

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv.c-271` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv.c::w32_service_thread_detach` |
| **Category** | concurrency |
| **Classification** | uncertain |
| **Confidence** | 40% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:271-301` |
| **Function** | `w32_service_thread_detach` |
| **Attack chain** | `proximity:prowl-concurrency-jv.c-271+prowl-concurrency-jv.c-330` (severity: high) |

#### Description

The function reads `tls_key_defs` under the lock, then releases the lock and uses the pointer `key_defs` without holding the lock. Between the unlock and the subsequent use of `key_defs` (including dereferencing it to access `keys_start_idx`, `keys_num`, `keys_dtors`, and `keys_next`), another thread could free or modify the `tls_key_defs` structure. Similarly, inside the loop, `key_defs->keys_next` is read under the lock but then the resulting pointer is used after the lock is released. This is a classic check-then-act / TOCTOU pattern.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to: (1) cause jq to run in a multi-threaded context on Windows where this DLL code is active, (2) trigger concurrent thread detachment and key definition modification/deletion, (3) win the race between the lock release and pointer dereference so that the tls_keys structure is freed or modified. The attacker would need to call a destructor through a corrupted function pointer for code execution.

#### Analysis

There is a genuine TOCTOU pattern here: `tls_key_defs` is read under the lock, then the lock is released, and the pointer is used without protection. Similarly, `key_defs->keys_next` is read under the lock but the resulting pointer is used after unlock. However, several factors make practical exploitability uncertain:

1. **Append-only data structure**: Looking at the code pattern, `tls_key_defs` appears to be a linked list of key definition blocks. In typical pthread_key_create emulation on Windows, these blocks are allocated and appended but never freed during the process lifetime. Keys are created but the backing storage is not deallocated until process exit. If the data structure is truly append-only (no deletion/freeing of tls_keys nodes), then the TOCTOU is benign because the pointer remains valid even after the lock is released.

2. **DllMain context**: `w32_service_thread_detach` is called from DllMain during DLL_THREAD_DETACH. During DllMain execution, the OS loader lock is held, which significantly constrains what other threads can do concurrently (they cannot load/unload DLLs, and new threads are blocked from starting). However, existing threads can still run and potentially call pthread_key_create which would modify the list.

3. **Race window**: The window between reading the pointer and using it is very small (a few instructions). An attacker would need to control concurrent thread behavior precisely.

4. **Attack surface**: This is Windows-specific TLS emulation code in jq (a command-line JSON processor). An attacker would need to trigger concurrent thread creation/destruction and key creation, which is not a typical attack vector for jq.

5. **Impact if exploitable**: If the race were won and a freed pointer were dereferenced, it could lead to use-after-free, potentially allowing code execution via a corrupted destructor function pointer. But without evidence that nodes are ever freed, this remains theoretical.

Without seeing the full lifecycle of `tls_key_defs` nodes (particularly whether they are ever freed), I cannot definitively classify this as exploitable or false_positive.

### 14. [MEDIUM] Incorrect Error Check on CreateSemaphore Return Value

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-17` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h::pthread_mutex_init` |
| **Category** | concurrency |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 82% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_thread.h:17-24` |
| **Function** | `pthread_mutex_init` |

#### Description

CreateSemaphore returns NULL on failure, not INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE. The function checks against INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE, which means a failed CreateSemaphore call will not be detected, and the mutex will be initialized with a NULL handle. Subsequent operations (WaitForSingleObject, ReleaseSemaphore) on a NULL handle will fail silently or behave unpredictably, potentially leading to a lack of mutual exclusion.

#### Attack Scenario

1. Attacker causes handle exhaustion on the target Windows system (e.g., by opening many handles through other interfaces). 2. jq initializes a pthread_mutex_t, which calls CreateSemaphore. 3. CreateSemaphore fails and returns NULL. 4. The incorrect check against INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE passes, and the mutex is 'initialized' with a NULL handle. 5. All subsequent lock/unlock operations on this mutex silently fail, providing no mutual exclusion. 6. Concurrent threads accessing shared data protected by this mutex can corrupt state, potentially leading to crashes or exploitable memory corruption.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis correctly identifies a real bug: `CreateSemaphore` returns `NULL` on failure (not `INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE`). The check `if (m->h == INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE)` will never catch a failed `CreateSemaphore` call. If `CreateSemaphore` fails, `m->h` will be `NULL`, and the function will return 0 (success). Subsequent calls to `WaitForSingleObject(NULL, INFINITE)` will return `WAIT_FAILED`, and `ReleaseSemaphore(NULL, 1, NULL)` will also fail. This means the mutex will provide no mutual exclusion at all - every thread will proceed as if it acquired the lock. However, the practical exploitability depends on whether `CreateSemaphore` actually fails in practice. On a healthy Windows system, creating a semaphore rarely fails unless the system is under extreme resource pressure (handle exhaustion). An attacker would need to cause handle exhaustion or similar resource depletion to trigger this path. That said, the bug is real and the consequence (complete loss of mutual exclusion) is significant. This is not a race condition per se - it's an incorrect error check that could lead to loss of synchronization. The concurrency impact is that if triggered, all critical sections protected by this mutex become unprotected, which could lead to data corruption, use-after-free, or other memory safety issues depending on what the mutex protects.

### 15. [MEDIUM] TOCTOU Race Condition in key_lookup and w32_service_thread_detach Due to Premature Mutex Release

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-48` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h::pthread_mutex_unlock` |
| **Category** | concurrency |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 45% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_thread.h:48-54` |
| **Function** | `pthread_mutex_unlock` |
| **Attack chain** | `proximity:prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-48+prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-26` (severity: high) |

#### Description

The key_lookup() and w32_service_thread_detach() functions read shared data (tls_key_defs, key_defs->keys_next) while holding the mutex, but then immediately release the mutex and continue to dereference the pointer outside the lock. This creates a TOCTOU race condition where another thread could modify or free the linked list nodes between the unlock and the subsequent use of the pointer.

#### Attack Scenario

1. Thread A calls pthread_key_create during DLL initialization, setting up key destructors.
2. Thread B is detaching (DLL_THREAD_DETACH triggers w32_service_thread_detach).
3. Thread B reads tls_key_defs under lock, releases lock, then traverses the list without the lock.
4. Concurrently, Thread A modifies keys_dtors[i] (e.g., marking a key as DEAD_KEY or creating a new key that overwrites a slot).
5. Thread B reads a stale or partially-written destructor function pointer and calls it, potentially leading to calling the wrong function or a corrupted pointer.
6. In a more adversarial scenario, if key deletion were implemented (freeing nodes), this would be a clear use-after-free.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis identifies a real TOCTOU race condition in key_lookup() and w32_service_thread_detach(). Both functions acquire the mutex, read a pointer from the shared linked list (tls_key_defs or keys_next), immediately release the mutex, and then dereference the pointer outside the lock. If another thread were to free or modify the linked list node between the unlock and the dereference, this could lead to use-after-free or reading stale/corrupted data.

However, the practical exploitability is limited by several factors:

1. **No pthread_key_delete that frees nodes**: Looking at the code, pthread_key_create only adds to the linked list (appending new nodes). There's no visible pthread_key_delete implementation that would free nodes. The linked list is append-only, meaning nodes are never removed or freed during normal operation. This significantly reduces the race window's impact since the pointer read under the lock will remain valid.

2. **keys_next is set once**: A node's keys_next pointer transitions from NULL to a valid pointer exactly once, and the node itself is never freed. So reading keys_next outside the lock after observing it under the lock is relatively safe - the worst case is reading a stale NULL when it has been updated, which would cause an assertion failure rather than memory corruption.

3. **Limited attack surface**: This is Windows-specific code (Win32 TLS emulation), and the race requires concurrent thread creation/destruction with key creation. In jq's typical usage pattern, keys are created during DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH (initialization), and thread detach happens later, so the race window is narrow.

4. **keys_dtors array mutation**: The more concerning race is that keys_dtors entries can be modified (set to DEAD_KEY or a new dtor) by one thread while another thread reads them outside the lock. This could lead to calling a stale destructor function pointer.

Despite the append-only nature reducing severity, the pattern of reading shared mutable state outside a lock is a genuine concurrency bug. The dtor function pointer could theoretically be read as stale, leading to calling a wrong destructor. The race window is small but real.

### 16. [MEDIUM] TOCTOU Race Condition in pthread_mutex_destroy via InterlockedCompareExchangePointer

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-55` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h::pthread_mutex_destroy` |
| **Category** | concurrency |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 62% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_thread.h:55-64` |
| **Function** | `pthread_mutex_destroy` |

#### Description

The InterlockedCompareExchangePointer on line 60 reads m->h as the comparand (third argument), but this read of m->h is NOT atomic with respect to the compare-and-swap operation itself. The value of m->h is first loaded (non-atomically) to be used as the comparand, and then the CAS operation compares the current value of m->h against this stale snapshot. Between the load of m->h for the comparand and the actual atomic CAS, another thread could change m->h, causing the CAS to fail (returning the current value without swapping). This means the function might fail to set m->h to INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE, and the old handle would not be closed — or worse, in a concurrent scenario, the handle could be closed while another thread is still using it.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to trigger concurrent calls to pthread_mutex_destroy (or destroy racing with init/lock) on the same mutex from multiple threads. If jq processes untrusted input that triggers multi-threaded operations sharing mutexes, the attacker could craft input that maximizes contention. Winning the race could cause handle leaks (DoS via resource exhaustion) or closing a handle that another thread is using (potential for handle confusion attacks on Windows).

#### Analysis

The TOCTOU race condition in pthread_mutex_destroy is real. The code reads m->h non-atomically to use as the comparand for InterlockedCompareExchangePointer. Between this read and the actual CAS operation, another thread could modify m->h (e.g., via pthread_mutex_init or another concurrent destroy). This creates two problematic scenarios: (1) The CAS fails because m->h changed, so the old handle is never closed (handle leak), and m->h is not set to INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE, leaving the mutex in an inconsistent state. (2) If another thread initializes a new handle into m->h between the non-atomic read and the CAS, the CAS could succeed with the wrong comparand, potentially closing a handle that another thread just created and is actively using (use-after-close / handle confusion). However, the practical exploitability depends on context. This is a Windows compatibility shim for pthreads in jq (a command-line JSON processor). The race window is small (between a register load and the CAS instruction), typically just a few instructions wide. In jq's typical usage patterns, concurrent mutex destruction is unlikely. But the bug is architecturally real - the correct pattern would be to use InterlockedExchangePointer to atomically swap m->h with INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE, then close the old handle. The security impact is moderate: handle leak or use-after-close could lead to denial of service or, in theory, handle reuse attacks on Windows where handle values get recycled.

### 17. [MEDIUM] Race condition in mutex initialization (double-checked locking without proper barriers)

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-26` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h::pthread_mutex_lock` |
| **Category** | concurrency |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 72% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_thread.h:26-46` |
| **Function** | `pthread_mutex_lock` |
| **Attack chain** | `func:/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_thread.h::pthread_mutex_lock` (severity: medium) |

#### Description

The pthread_mutex_lock implementation has a race condition during mutex initialization. The initial read of m->h using InterlockedCompareExchangePointer(&m->h, m->h, m->h) is a read-modify-write that reads m->h twice non-atomically for the comparand and exchange parameters. Two threads can both see h as NULL/INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE simultaneously, both create new semaphores, and while the second InterlockedCompareExchangePointer prevents double-initialization of the handle, the thread that loses the race (created=0) proceeds to WaitForSingleObject. However, the winning thread (created=1) never calls WaitForSingleObject and considers itself to have acquired the lock. This means the winning thread enters the critical section without actually acquiring the semaphore, so if a third thread later releases the semaphore (via ReleaseSemaphore in the corresponding unlock), the losing thread will wake up and enter the critical section simultaneously with the winner — but only if the winner hasn't released yet. More critically, the initial read `InterlockedCompareExchangePointer(&m->h, m->h, m->h)` evaluates m->h for the comparand and exchange value arguments before the atomic operation, meaning the value of m->h could change between argument evaluation and the actual CAS, leading to the CAS writing a stale value back.

#### Attack Scenario

1. Multiple threads call pthread_key_create or other functions that lock tls_key_defs_lock simultaneously during DLL initialization or thread detach. 2. Two or more threads hit the lazy initialization path of the mutex concurrently. 3. Due to the non-atomic read of m->h for CAS arguments, one thread's CAS writes back a stale NULL value over a valid semaphore handle. 4. This causes re-initialization of the mutex, allowing a second thread to also consider itself the lock holder. 5. Both threads enter the critical section simultaneously, corrupting the tls_key_defs linked list. 6. This can lead to use-after-free when traversing the corrupted list, or double-free of TLS destructors.

#### Analysis

The pthread_mutex_lock implementation has a genuine race condition in the lazy initialization path. The key issue is the line `h = InterlockedCompareExchangePointer(&m->h, m->h, m->h)` where `m->h` is evaluated multiple times as function arguments before the atomic operation executes. The C calling convention evaluates arguments in unspecified order, and the value of `m->h` can change between when it's read for the comparand/exchange parameters and when the CAS actually executes. This means the CAS could write back a stale value.

More importantly, when two threads race to initialize the mutex: Thread A creates a semaphore with initial count 0, wins the CAS, sets `created=1`, and skips WaitForSingleObject - entering the critical section without actually acquiring the semaphore. Thread B loses the CAS, sets `created=0`, and calls WaitForSingleObject on the semaphore (which has count 0, so it blocks). This part actually works correctly for the two-thread case - Thread A holds the lock, Thread B waits.

However, the fundamental problem is that the mutex semantics are broken: the semaphore is created with initial count 0, meaning the first thread to initialize it considers itself the lock holder without decrementing any counter. If Thread A unlocks (ReleaseSemaphore increments count to 1), Thread B wakes up. This seems correct for the basic case.

The real vulnerability is the non-atomic read of `m->h` for the CAS arguments. If `m->h` changes between argument evaluation and CAS execution, the CAS could overwrite a valid handle with a stale/NULL value, causing the mutex to be re-initialized and allowing multiple threads to simultaneously believe they hold the lock. This leads to data races on the protected data structures (tls_key_defs linked list), potentially causing use-after-free or corruption.

### 18. [MEDIUM] CreateSemaphore failure check uses wrong error value

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-26` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h::pthread_mutex_lock` |
| **Category** | concurrency |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 85% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_thread.h:26-46` |
| **Function** | `pthread_mutex_lock` |
| **Attack chain** | `proximity:prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-26+prowl-concurrency-jv_thread.h-26` (severity: medium) |

#### Description

On Windows, CreateSemaphore returns NULL on failure, not INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE. The code checks `if (new_h == INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE)` which will never be true on failure, causing the function to proceed with a NULL handle, leading to undefined behavior when WaitForSingleObject is called with NULL.

#### Attack Scenario

1. Trigger resource exhaustion on Windows (e.g., exhaust kernel handle limit) so CreateSemaphore returns NULL. 2. Call into jq functionality that triggers pthread_mutex_lock on tls_key_defs_lock. 3. CreateSemaphore fails, returns NULL, but the check against INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE passes. 4. NULL is stored as the mutex handle via InterlockedCompareExchangePointer. 5. All subsequent mutex lock/unlock operations on this mutex are no-ops (WaitForSingleObject with NULL returns immediately with WAIT_FAILED). 6. Multiple threads can now concurrently access tls_key_defs without synchronization, leading to data races, memory corruption, or use-after-free.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis is correct: CreateSemaphore on Windows returns NULL on failure, not INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE. The code at line 36 checks `if (new_h == INVALID_HANDLE_VALUE)` which will never be true when CreateSemaphore fails. This means on failure, the code proceeds with a NULL handle. If the InterlockedCompareExchangePointer succeeds (stores NULL into m->h), subsequent calls to WaitForSingleObject with a NULL handle will fail, and the mutex will not provide mutual exclusion. If the ICEP fails (because another thread already set the handle), the code calls CloseHandle(NULL) which is benign (returns FALSE), and then falls through to WaitForSingleObject(m->h, INFINITE) which would use whatever handle the other thread set - this path is actually fine. The critical issue is when CreateSemaphore fails AND the ICEP succeeds: the mutex handle becomes NULL, WaitForSingleObject(NULL, INFINITE) returns WAIT_FAILED immediately, and the function returns 0 (success) without actually acquiring the lock. This breaks mutual exclusion for all subsequent users of this mutex, leading to data races on the protected tls_key_defs structure. While CreateSemaphore failure is uncommon under normal conditions, it can occur under resource exhaustion. This is not a race condition per se but a bug in error handling that breaks the concurrency primitive. The impact is loss of mutual exclusion protecting shared data structures, which could lead to memory corruption or use-after-free in the TLS key management code.

### 19. [MEDIUM] Off-by-one in end rounding logic can exceed array/string length

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv_aux.c-19` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv_aux.c::parse_slice` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 82% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_aux.c:19-78` |
| **Function** | `parse_slice` |

#### Description

The rounding adjustment `end += end < dend ? 1 : 0` on line 69 is applied AFTER the bounds check `if (end > len) end = len` on line 68. This means if `dend` is a non-integer value like `len + 0.5`, `end` will be truncated to `len` by the cast, pass the `end > len` check (since `end == len`), but then get incremented to `len + 1` by the rounding adjustment (since `len < len + 0.5`). This violates the assertion on line 72 and causes out-of-bounds access in downstream functions like `jv_array_slice` or `jv_string_slice`.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker would need to provide a non-integer slice end value like `len + 0.5` to trigger the off-by-one. However, the guard condition `end < len` on line 69 prevents the increment when `end == len`, making the described attack path non-functional.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis identifies a real off-by-one bug in the `parse_slice` function. Let me trace through the logic carefully:

1. `dend` is a double from user input (e.g., `len + 0.5` where `len` is the array/string length)
2. Line 67: `end = dend > INT_MAX ? INT_MAX : (int)dend;` — casting `len + 0.5` to int gives `len` (truncation toward zero)
3. Line 68: `if (end > len) end = len;` — `end == len`, so this check passes (no clamping needed)
4. Line 69: `if (end < len) end += end < dend ? 1 : 0;` — `end == len` which is NOT less than `len`, so this condition is FALSE. The increment does NOT happen.

Wait, let me re-examine. If `dend = len + 0.5`, then `end = len` after cast, `end > len` is false so no clamping, and `end < len` is also false, so the rounding line doesn't execute. The bug doesn't trigger in this case.

But consider `dend = len - 0.5`. Then `end = len - 1` after cast. `end > len` is false. `end < len` is true. `end < dend`? `len-1 < len-0.5` is true. So `end` becomes `len`, which is valid.

What about `dend = len - 0.001`? `end = len - 1` (truncation). `end < len` is true. `end < dend`? `len-1 < len-0.001` is true. `end` becomes `len`. Still valid.

Actually, for the bug to trigger, we need `end == len` after the cast but `dend > len`. If `dend = len + 0.5`, `(int)dend = len` (for positive len). Then `end > len` is false (end == len). `end < len` is false. So no increment. The assertion holds.

However, consider negative zero edge cases or when `len = 0` and `dend` is something like `0.5`. `end = 0`, `end > 0` false, `end < 0` false. No increment. Still safe.

Actually, I need to reconsider. The real concern is: can we get `end = len` and then have the rounding increment it? The condition `end < len` prevents this when `end == len`. So the specific scenario described in the hypothesis doesn't actually work because the rounding line has the guard `end < len`.

But wait — what if `len` itself overflows or there's an integer issue? For normal cases, the guard `end < len` prevents the off-by-one when `end == len`.

Re-reading more carefully: the hypothesis says end gets set to len by the bounds check, then the rounding adds 1. But the rounding line checks `end < len` first, and if `end == len`, it won't execute. The hypothesis appears to be wrong about the execution flow.

However, there could be edge cases with very large values or specific floating point behavior. But for the standard case described, this is a false positive.

### 20. [MEDIUM] Stack overflow via deeply nested arrays/objects in recursive jv_cmp

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv_aux.c-600` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv_aux.c::jv_cmp` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | exploitable |
| **Confidence** | 82% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv_aux.c:600-672` |
| **Function** | `jv_cmp` |

#### Description

The jv_cmp function is recursive. When comparing arrays (line 638-650) or objects (line 653-667), it calls itself recursively on element values. For deeply nested arrays or objects (e.g., [[[[...]]]] thousands of levels deep), this will cause unbounded stack recursion leading to a stack overflow crash.

#### Attack Scenario

1. Attacker crafts a deeply nested JSON array, e.g., `[[[[...]]]]` with 10,000+ levels of nesting. 2. The input is processed by jq with an expression that triggers jv_cmp on the nested structure (e.g., `sort`, `min`, `max`, comparison operators like `<`, `<=`, `>=`, `>`, or `bsearch`). 3. jv_cmp recursively descends into each nesting level, consuming stack space for each level. 4. The stack is exhausted, causing a segmentation fault and crashing the jq process (denial of service).

#### Analysis

The jv_cmp function is genuinely recursive when comparing arrays and objects. For arrays, at line 650, it calls `jv_cmp(xa, xb)` on array elements. If those elements are themselves arrays (deeply nested), this creates unbounded recursion proportional to the nesting depth. An attacker who can provide input to jq (e.g., a deeply nested JSON like `[[[[...]]]]` with thousands of levels) can trigger a stack overflow. The listed sanitizers (bounds checking, parameterized query placeholders, value clamping, length/size checks) do not address recursion depth - none of them limit the nesting depth of the input JSON or the recursion depth of jv_cmp. Stack canaries would detect stack buffer overflows but not stack exhaustion from deep recursion, which typically results in a segfault/SIGSEGV rather than a controlled overwrite. This is a denial-of-service vulnerability. While it's a crash rather than arbitrary code execution, it's still exploitable for DoS. The attacker has full control over the nesting depth through crafted JSON input. Any jq expression that triggers comparison of deeply nested structures (sort, min, max, comparison operators, bsearch, etc.) would hit this path.

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c-330` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_key_create` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | false_positive |
| **Confidence** | 85% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:330-402` |
| **Function** | `pthread_key_create` |

#### Description

In the else branch (decpt > 0), after copying the significant digits, the code pads with zeros: `for(; decpt > 0; decpt--) *b++ = '0';`. If `decpt` is much larger than the number of significant digits (e.g., for a number like 1e308), this could write up to ~308 '0' characters. The caller allocates `JVP_DTOA_FMT_MAX_LEN` bytes. If `JVP_DTOA_FMT_MAX_LEN` is not large enough to accommodate this (e.g., 1 digit + 308 zeros + null = 310 bytes), a buffer overflow occurs.

#### Attack Scenario

If JVP_DTOA_FMT_MAX_LEN is too small (less than ~35 bytes), formatting a double with many trailing zeros in the non-scientific notation path could overflow the stack buffer. The attacker would provide a JSON number that triggers this path.

#### Analysis

The provided code is `pthread_key_create`, a TLS key management function. It has no relation to dtoa formatting, decimal point handling, or buffer writing with '0' padding. The hypothesis describes a vulnerability in completely different code than what is shown. Cannot validate the claim against the provided source.

| Field | Value |

| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_key_create` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | false_positive |
| **Confidence** | 85% |

| **Function** | `pthread_key_create` |

#### Description

However, the write index `ri` is declared as `uint32_t`, which can only hold values up to ~4.29 billion. If the input string is large enough that all bytes require percent-encoding (3 bytes each), `ri` will wrap around past UINT32_MAX, causing writes to earlier positions in the already-allocated buffer (data corruption) and an incorrect length passed to `jv_string_sized`.

An attacker provides a ~1.5GB string of non-ASCII bytes to the @uri format. The buffer is correctly allocated at ~4.5GB, but the uint32_t index `ri` wraps around after ~4.29 billion, causing writes to wrap to the beginning of the buffer and producing corrupted output. The `jv_string_sized(result, ri)` call uses the wrapped value, potentially creating an incorrectly sized string.

#### Analysis

The provided code is `pthread_key_create`, a TLS key management function. It has no relation to URI encoding, percent-encoding, or uint32_t index variables. The hypothesis describes a vulnerability in completely different code than what is shown. Cannot validate the claim against the provided source.

|-------|-------|

| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_key_create` |

| **Function** | `pthread_key_create` |

#### Description

If either Bigint `a` or `b` has `wds == 0`, the function computes `i - j` which could be 0 if both are 0. In that case, `j == 0`, so `xa = xa0 + 0` and `xb = xb0 + 0`. The loop then executes `*--xa` and `*--xb`, which decrements the pointers before the start of the `x` array, reading out-of-bounds memory. Even if only one has `wds == 0` and the other doesn't, the early return via `i -= j` would handle it, but the case where both are 0 leads to an underflow read.

#### Attack Scenario

An attacker provides input to jq that triggers a floating-point conversion path where a Bigint with wds=0 is compared via cmp(). This causes reads before the x[] array, potentially leaking adjacent heap data or causing a crash.

#### Analysis

The hypothesis describes a vulnerability in completely different code than what is shown. Cannot validate the claim against the provided source.

### 27. [MEDIUM] Use-after-free via destructor call on old value during pthread_setspecific

| Field | Value |
|-------|-------|
| **ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c-330` |
| **Stable ID** | `prowl-memory-jv.c::pthread_key_create` |
| **Category** | memory |
| **Classification** | uncertain |
| **Confidence** | 50% |
| **File** | `/Users/toniantunovic/dev/voldeq/lucidshark-code/prowl-test-projects/jq/src/jv.c:330-402` |
| **Function** | `pthread_key_create` |
| **Attack chain** | `proximity:prowl-concurrency-jv.c-271+prowl-memory-jv.c-330` (severity: high) |

#### Description

On line 471-472, when setting a new value, the old value's destructor is called (`dtor(values.values[key])`), and then the new value is stored. If the destructor itself calls `pthread_setspecific` for the same key (re-entrant call), the `values.values` pointer or the value at `values.values[key]` could be modified during the destructor call, leading to use-after-free or double-free scenarios.

#### Attack Scenario

A caller calls `pthread_setspecific(key, NULL)` after already manually calling the destructor on the old value. The implementation sees the old value is non-NULL and calls the destructor again, resulting in a double-free. Looking at `tsd_fini`, it calls `pthread_setspecific(nomem_handler_key, NULL)` before `free(nomem_handler)`, which means the implementation's destructor would be called on the still-valid pointer, and then `free` is called again — a double-free.

#### Analysis

While the provided code shows `pthread_key_create` and not `pthread_setspecific`, the hypothesis describes a reentrancy issue in the related `pthread_setspecific` function from the same TLS emulation layer. The concern about a destructor calling `pthread_setspecific` for the same key is a legitimate design concern in custom pthread TLS implementations. However, without seeing the actual `pthread_setspecific` code, and since the shown code doesn't contain the described vulnerability pattern, this cannot be confirmed.

This is not thread-safe: if two threads call my_mktime concurrently, they can corrupt each other's TZ setting, leading to incorrect time calculations or crashes.
